# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Java Service Initialization Lifecycle Pattern in Kafka

## Your Task

Find all Java source files in apache/kafka that implement the Startable/Stoppable service lifecycle: the Lifecycle interface (or equivalent), the service startup ordering, and the coordinated shutdown sequence that ensures dependent services stop in reverse order.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the crossorg domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/kafka--0753c489, sg-evals/flink--0cc95fcc, sg-evals/camel--1006f047.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/kafka--0753c489` (apache/kafka)
- `sg-evals/flink--0cc95fcc` (apache/flink)
- `sg-evals/camel--1006f047` (apache/camel)

62-assistanttext-
Perfect! I've successfully created a comprehensive analysis of the Java service initialization lifecycle pattern in Apache Kafka. Here's a summary of what I found:

## Key Findings:

**Service Lifecycle Pattern:**
Apache Kafka implements a hierarchical, coordinated lifecycle management system where:

1. **Forward Initialization Order** (startup):
   - Worker → Status Backing Store → Config Backing Store
   - Each service depends on previous ones being initialized

2. **Reverse Shutdown Order** (graceful shutdown):
   - Config Backing Store → Status Backing Store → Worker
   - Services are stopped in reverse dependency order

3. **Main Components:**
   - **Herder interface** - defines start()/stop() contract
   - **AbstractHerder** - provides startServices()/stopServices() template methods
   - **DistributedHerder** - implements distributed coordination with group membership
   - **Connect class** - top-level orchestrator managing Herder, REST server, and shutdown hooks

4. **Backing Stores** - all follow the same lifecycle pattern:
   - OffsetBackingStore (Kafka/File/Memory implementations)
   - ConfigBackingStore (Kafka/Memory implementations)
   - StatusBackingStore (Kafka/Memory implementations)

5. **Coordinated Shutdown:**
   - Distributed nodes coordinate via consumer group protocol
   - Graceful shutdown with configurable timeouts (60+ seconds)
   - Extension points for custom resource cleanup via `uponShutdown` list

The complete analysis with all file paths, symbols, and detailed narrative explanation has been saved to `/workspace/answer.json`.

For example, in distributed mode, an implementation of this interface\\n45:  * knows how to accept a connector configuration, may need to route it to the current leader worker for the cluster so\\n46:  * the config can be written to persistent storage, and then ensures the new connector is correctly instantiated on one\\n47:  * of the workers.\\n48:  * </p>\\n49:  * <p>\\n50:  * This class must implement all the actions that can be taken on the cluster (add/remove connectors, pause/resume tasks,\\n51:  * get state of connectors and tasks, etc). The non-Java interfaces to the cluster (REST API and CLI) are very simple\\n52:  * wrappers of the functionality provided by this interface.\\n53:  * </p>\\n54:  * <p>\\n55:  * In standalone mode, the implementation of this interface will be trivial because no coordination is needed. generalized\n134:  *     group/consumer coordinator). Each instance of DistributedHerder joins the group and indicates what its current\n135:  *     configuration state is (where it is in the configuration log). The group coordinator selects one member to take\n136:  *     this information and assign each instance a subset of the active connectors & tasks to execute. The assignment\n137:  *     strategy depends on the {@link ConnectAssignor} used. Once an assignment is received, the DistributedHerder simply\n138:  *     runs its assigned connectors and tasks in a {@link Worker}.\n139:  * </p>\n140:  * <p>\n141:  *     In addition to distributing work, the DistributedHerder uses the leader determined during the work assignment\n142:  *     to select a leader for this generation of the group who is responsible for other tasks that can only be performed\n143:  *     by a single node at a time. Most importantly, this includes writing updated configurations for connectors and tasks,\n144:  *     (and therefore, also for creating, destroy, and scaling up/down connectors).\n145:  * </p>\n146:  * <p>\n147:  *     The DistributedHerder uses a single thread for most of its processing. This includes processing\n148:  *     config changes, handling task rebalances and serving requests from the HTTP layer. The latter are pushed\n149:  *     into a queue until the thread has time to handle them. A consequence of this is that requests can get blocked\n150:  *     behind a worker rebalance. When the herder knows that a rebalance is expected, it typically returns an error\n151:  *     immediately to the request, but this is not always possible (in particular when another worker has requested\n152:  *     the rebalance). Similar to handling HTTP requests, config changes which are observed asynchronously by polling\n153:  *     the config log are batched for handling in the work thread.\n154:  * </p>\n155:  */\n156: This ensures dependent services are ready before dependents are initialized.\\n\\n2. **Graceful Shutdown**: Shutdown operations use configurable timeouts (e.g., GRACEFUL_SHUTDOWN_TIMEOUT_MS = 60 seconds in RestServer) to allow in-flight requests to complete.\\n\\n3. **Coordinated Shutdown**: DistributedHerder coordinates shutdown across distributed nodes using the group membership protocol, ensuring proper cleanup.\\n\\n4. **Lifecycle Hooks**: `startServices()` and `stopServices()` serve as extension points for subclasses to customize initialization/shutdown.\\n\\n5. **Resource Cleanup**: The `uponShutdown` list in DistributedHerder allows external components to register cleanup actions that execute at shutdown time.\\n\\n**MirrorMaker Integration:**\\n\\nMirrorMaker (connect/mirror/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/mirror/MirrorMaker.java) demonstrates the lifecycle pattern at a higher level, managing multiple herders and coordinating their startup/shutdown.\\n\\nThis lifecycle management ensures that Kafka Connect can reliably initialize complex, distributed systems while guaranteeing that all resources are properly cleaned up during shutdown, preventing resource leaks and data loss.\"\n}\n",
